The Eight Deviations and the Objection of Opposite Qualities
The author states eight deviations, faces an objection, and answers with the concept of proportion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the number of deviations from temperament is derived, why an objection suggests eighty possibilities, and how the concept of proportion resolves it.

Core Idea
Proportion, not absolute quantity, preserves moderation.

After establishing the real temperament, the author turns to deviations. He states they are eight, analogous to the real. But an objection arises: since deviation can involve two opposite qualities increasing or decreasing, why not eighty? The answer lies in understanding 'due proportion' — the key to maintaining moderation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a statement, an objection, and a detailed answer.
2
Understand the eight deviations analogy
The author says deviations are confined to eight, by analogy with the real temperament. Reflect on why eight: because there are four qualities, and each can be excessive or deficient individually, plus combinations? But the objection challenges this.
3
Grasp the objection: why eighty?
The objection argues that since two opposite qualities (like heat and cold) can both increase or both decrease relative to the due amount, there are many more possibilities: four qualities × two directions (increase/decrease) × combinations = 80. Try to see the reasoning.
The objection presumes that any change in absolute amount breaks moderation.
4
Analyze the answer: proportion preserved
The author answers by defining 'due proportion': the ratio between active qualities (heat and cold) and between passive qualities (moisture and dryness) must be suitable. As long as this ratio is maintained, moderation holds even if absolute amounts change. Study the example given.
This is the key insight: proportion matters more than quantity.
5
Consider the nuance about personal temperament
The author adds a nuance: in personal temperament, individual organs can deviate differently, e.g., heart hotter, brain colder. This does not break the eight-fold classification because there is no single intermediate quality to maintain. Understand that this is an exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (والخارج عن هذا الاعتدال أيضاً ينحصر في ثمانية . على قياس الحقيقي. واعترض بأن الخروج بالمتضادين ممكن ههنا بأن يزيدا أو ينقصا من القدر اللائق فيجوز الخروج بكيفية أو كيفيتين أو ثلاث أو أربع بحسب الزيادة والنقصان جمعاً وإفراداً يصير ثمانين . وأجيب : بأن معنى توفر القسط اللائق أن يكون بين الفاعلتين نسبة تليق بالممتزج، وكذا بين المنفعلتين، فما دامت النسبة محفوظة، فالاعتدال بحاله سواء زادت المقادير أو انتقصت كما إذا كان اللائق عشرة أجزاء من الحرارة، وخمسة من البرودة، فصارت الحرارة اثني عشر أو ثمانية والبرودة ستة أو أربعة، وإن صارت البرودة ستة، والحرارة أحد عشر فهو أبرد مما ينبغي لا أحر ، أو ثلاثة عشر فأحرلا أبرد . نعم لا يبعد ذلك في الاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الداخل بأن يصير مزاج قلب زيد أحر ، وأيبس من أعدل أحواله ومزاج دماغه أبرد وأرطب وبالعكس. إذ ليس هناك كيفية متوسطة تبقى مع حفظ التسمية ] .
He said: 'And that which deviates from this moderation is also confined to eight, by analogy with the real. And it was objected: deviation by two opposites is possible here, in that they both increase or fall short of the due amount, so deviation by one quality, or two, or three, or four is permissible according to increase and decrease, collectively and individually, yielding eighty. And it was answered: the meaning of the sufficiency of the due share is that between the two active qualities there is a proportion suitable to the thing mixed, and likewise between the two passive qualities; so as long as the proportion is preserved, moderation remains as it is, whether the amounts increase or decrease. For example, if the due amount is ten parts of heat and five of cold, and heat becomes twelve or eight, and cold six or four, then if cold becomes six and heat becomes eleven, it is colder than it should be, not hotter; or if thirteen, then hotter, not colder. Indeed, that is not unlikely in personal temperament in relation to the interior: that the temperament of Zayd's heart becomes hotter and drier than its most balanced state, and the temperament of his brain colder and moister, and vice versa, for there is no intermediate quality that remains while preserving the name.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents a standard classification and an objection. It begins by stating that deviations from the ideal temperament are also confined to eight types, similar to the real temperament. An objection is raised: since deviation can occur with two opposite qualities (e.g., heat and cold both increasing or both decreasing), the number of possible deviations could be much larger—eighty in total. The author then answers: the meaning of 'due proportion' (tawafur al-qist al-la'iq) is that the ratio between active qualities (heat and cold) and between passive qualities (moisture and dryness) must be suitable for the mixture. As long as that proportion is preserved, moderation remains even if absolute amounts change. He gives an example: if the due is ten heat to five cold, then if both increase proportionally (12:6 or 8:4) moderation is maintained; if they deviate from this ratio, then it becomes hotter or colder. He then notes a nuance: in the personal temperament, it is possible for individual organs to deviate differently (e.g., heart hotter, brain colder) without preserving a single intermediate quality, but that does not break the classification of eight overall types of deviation.

Key Terms
خروج عن الاعتدال
متضادين
توفر القسط اللائق
نسبة
قدر لائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any increase or decrease in a quality destroys moderation.
Correction: The author clarifies that as long as the proportion between active and passive qualities remains suitable, moderation is preserved even if absolute amounts change.

Checkpoint
What is the key factor in determining whether a temperament remains moderate according to the answer given?
A) The absolute amount of heat and cold must be equal.
B) The ratio between active qualities and between passive qualities must be suitable.
C) The amounts of all four qualities must be identical.
D) The qualities must be exactly at their due amounts.

Key Takeaway
Moderation is defined by the proportion between active and passive qualities, not by absolute quantities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, do you often measure your devotion by absolute quantities—hours of prayer, chapters of Quran—while neglecting the proportion between your outward acts and inner sincerity? What specific step will you take this week to restore that proportion?
?
Think of a relationship where you have been trying to increase kindness or decrease anger in absolute terms, but the proportion between giving and expecting has been off. What is one concrete change you can make today to realign that ratio?
?
The author says moderation remains even when amounts change as long as proportion is preserved. Where in your practice of Islam have you added more effort but lost balance? Commit to one action that re-establishes the due proportion between that effort and your other responsibilities.

Al-Katibi's Enumeration of Deviations
Al-Katibi objects to the eightfold classification and systematically enumerates deviations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Katibi's objection to the simple eightfold classification and his systematic enumeration of all possible deviations from the assumed temperament.

Core Idea
Opposite qualities can both deviate; the measure is the due amount, not competition.

In his commentary on al-Mulakhkhas, al-Katibi challenges the standard medical view that deviations from the assumed temperament are limited to eight types. He argues that two opposite qualities can both exceed or fall short of the due amount, leading to many more possibilities. This passage presents his systematic enumeration, showing that the number of possible deviations is far greater than eight.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eightfold classification
Review the initial classification: deviations with one quality (4 directions) or two non-opposite qualities (4 combinations). This is the standard view al-Katibi objects to.
The passage begins by stating this classification before introducing the objection.
2
Understand al-Katibi's objection
Notice that al-Katibi allows two opposite qualities to deviate together. The key point is that the standard only considered increase of one over the other, but he considers increase or decrease relative to the due amount.
This is the core of his objection.
3
Follow the enumeration for one quality
He first gives the number for one quality: 8 (4 qualities × 2 directions). Confirm this matches the standard count.
4
Enumerate two qualities
He identifies 6 unordered pairs of qualities (including opposites). For each pair, there are 4 states: both increase, both decrease, first increase second decrease, first decrease second increase. Total 24.
This is the key expansion beyond the standard view.
5
Note the incomplete enumeration for three and four qualities
The text begins to discuss three qualities but cuts off. Recognize that al-Katibi intended to cover all cases, but the manuscript is incomplete.
This lesson covers only the available portion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني الاعتدال الفرضي المعتبر بحسب الطب ينحصر في ثمانية، لأنه إما أن يكون بكيفية واحدة من الأربع، فيكون أحر مما ينبغي، أو أبرد أو أرطب أو أيبس، وإما بكيفيتين غير متضادتين فيكون أحر وأرطب، أو أيبس أو أبرد وأرطب أو أيبس، واعترض الكاتبي في شرح الملخص . بأن الخروج عن هذا الاعتدال بكيفيتين متضادتين ممكن بأن تزيد الحرارة والبرودة جميعاً على القدر اللائق بالممتزج، أو تنقصا عنه، وكذا الرطوبة واليبوسة، ولا يلزم من ذلك كون المتضادتين غالبتين ومغلوبتين معاً في الخارج عن الاعتدال الحقيقي، لأن المعتبر ثمة زيادة كل على الأخرى. وههنا على القدر اللائق ، لا على الأخرى. وإذا جاز ذلك فالخروج إما أن يكون بكيفية، أو كيفيتين أو ثلاث كيفيات أو الكيفيات الأربع جميعاً والأول ثمانية أقسام حاصلة من ضرب أربعة أعني الكيفيات في اثنين . أعني الزيادة والنقصان والثاني: أربعة وعشرون قسماً لأن الكيفيتين الخارجتين، إما الحرارة مع البرودة أو مع الرطوبة أو مع اليبوسة. وإما البرودة مع الرطوبة أو مع اليبوسة ، وإما الرطوبة مع اليبوسة، فهذه ستة نضربها في أربع حالات، هي زيادة الكيفيتين ونقصانهما، وزيادة الأولى مع نقصان الثانية وبالعكس. والثالث اثنان
That is, the assumed temperament considered according to medicine is confined to eight, because either it is with one of the four qualities, so it is hotter than it should be, or colder, or moister, or drier; or with two non-opposite qualities, so it is hotter and moister, or hotter and drier, or colder and moister, or colder and drier. And al-Katibi objected in his commentary on al-Mulakhkhas: that deviation from this temperament with two opposite qualities is possible, in that heat and cold both exceed the due amount for the mixture, or both fall short of it, and likewise moisture and dryness; and it does not follow from that that the two opposites are both predominant and both subdued together in deviation from the real temperament, because what is considered there is the increase of each over the other, while here it is the due amount, not over the other. And if that is allowed, then deviation is either with one quality, or two, or three, or all four. The first: eight divisions resulting from multiplying four (i.e., the qualities) by two (i.e., increase and decrease). The second: twenty-four divisions, because the two exiting qualities are either heat with cold, or with moisture, or with dryness; or cold with moisture or with dryness; or moisture with dryness; these are six, and we multiply them by four states: increase of both qualities, decrease of both, increase of the first with decrease of the second, and vice versa. The third: two ...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage first explains the eightfold classification of deviations from the 'assumed temperament' (al-i'tidal al-fardi) as per medicine: either one quality (hotter, colder, moister, drier) or two non-opposite qualities (e.g., hotter and moister, etc.). Then al-Katibi objects: deviation with two opposite qualities (e.g., heat and cold both increasing) is possible, because the criterion here is the due amount (al-qadr al-la'iq) relative to the mixture, not the competition between the two qualities. He then systematically enumerates all possibilities: for one quality: 8 (4 qualities × 2 directions). For two qualities (including opposites): first, the pairs of qualities: there are 6 unordered pairs (heat-cold, heat-moisture, heat-dryness, cold-moisture, cold-dryness, moisture-dryness). For each pair, there are 4 states: both increase, both decrease, first increase second decrease, first decrease second increase. That gives 6 × 4 = 24. The next part (three qualities) begins but is cut off after stating 'the third: two' likely indicating two possibilities for three qualities, and presumably four qualities would be one case. This detailed objection shows that the simple eightfold classification is insufficient if opposite qualities can both exceed or fall short.

Key Terms
اعتدال فرضي
كيفيتين متضادتين
قدر لائق
ضرب
أقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposite qualities cannot both deviate simultaneously; the only possible deviations are with one quality or two non-opposite qualities.
Correction: Al-Katibi shows that two opposite qualities can both increase or both decrease relative to the due amount, leading to many more combinations.

Checkpoint
According to al-Katibi, how many combinations are there for deviation with two qualities (including opposites)?
A) 6
B) 8
C) 24
D) 4

Key Takeaway
Al-Katibi's enumeration shows that the possible deviations from the assumed temperament are far more numerous than the simple eightfold classification, totaling at least 8 (one quality) + 24 (two qualities) plus further cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, have you ever assumed a simple binary classification (e.g., 'sinful' vs 'righteous') that might be incomplete? What 'due amount' of virtues might you be neglecting by focusing on extremes?
?
This passage values precision in defining the criterion ('due amount' vs 'competition'). Where in your understanding of Islamic teachings have you accepted an incomplete or imprecise criterion? What would it take to examine all possibilities?
?
Al-Katibi's objection reveals that the standard classification was too limited. When you reflect on your own character, do you tend to limit your self-assessment to a few categories, missing the complexity of multiple qualities? How can you apply the idea of 'enumerating all possibilities' to your own inner state?

Personal Moderation in Medical Temperament
Explains personal moderation with an example of organ-specific balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that personal moderation in medical temperament is relative to each organ and that there is no single intermediate quality that preserves the definition of moderation universally.

Core Idea
Moderation in temperament is not fixed; it varies by organ and function.

This passage introduces the concept of 'personal moderation' (i'tidal shakhsī). Before defining pathological departures, we must understand that what is 'balanced' can differ between organs. The author illustrates this with an example of Zayd's heart and brain to show that each organ has its own moderate state.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Notice the example about Zayd's heart and brain.
The passage uses the word 'أبرد' (colder) which connects to the previous context.
2
Understand the example
The example shows that Zayd's heart can be hotter and drier than its own balanced state, while his brain is colder and moister than its balanced state – yet each can still be considered moderate for that organ.
This illustrates the relativity of personal moderation.
3
Grasp the key principle
The author states that there is no single intermediate quality that remains while preserving the name of moderation. This means moderation cannot be defined by a fixed absolute value.
This principle is fundamental for understanding medical temperaments.
4
Connect to medical temperament theory
Personal moderation is the baseline for each individual's health. It prepares the ground for discussing hypothetical moderation and departures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبرد . نعم لا يبعد ذلك في الاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الداخل بأن يصير مزاج قلب زيد أحر ، وأيبس من أعدل أحواله ومزاج دماغه أبرد وأرطب وبالعكس. إذ ليس هناك كيفية متوسطة تبقى مع حفظ التسمية . يعني الاعتدال الفرضي المعتبر بحسب الطب ينحصر في ثمانية، لأنه إما أن يكون بكيفية واحدة من الأربع، فيكون أحر مما ينبغي، أو أبرد أو أرطب أو أيبس.
Colder. Yes, that is not far-fetched in personal moderation relative to the interior, such that the temperament of Zayd's heart becomes hotter and drier than the most balanced of its states, and the temperament of his brain becomes colder and moister, and vice versa. For there is no intermediate quality that remains while preserving the name [of moderation]. He means: the hypothetical moderation considered in medicine is limited to eight, because it is either with one of the four qualities, so it is hotter than it should be, or colder, or moister, or drier.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'i'tidal (moderation) in medical temperament. It begins with an example showing that personal moderation (i'tidal shakhsī) can vary between organs: Zayd's heart may be relatively hot and dry, while his brain is cold and moist, each still being moderate for that organ. The key point is that there is no single intermediate quality that preserves the name of moderation universally. Then the author defines hypothetical moderation (i'tidal fardī) as considered in medicine: it is limited to eight types of departure, each involving a single quality being excessive or deficient—too hot, too cold, too moist, or too dry. Thus the simplest classification yields eight cases (4 qualities × 2 directions).

Key Terms
الاعتدال الشخصي (al-iʿtidāl al-shakhṣī)
كيفية (kayfiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can Zayd's heart and brain have opposite temperaments yet both be considered moderate?
A) Because moderation is defined by the same intermediate quality for both organs
B) Because there is no intermediate quality that remains while preserving the name of moderation
C) Because the heart is always hotter than the brain
D) Because the passage does not explain this

Key Takeaway
Personal moderation is relative to each organ and cannot be defined by a single fixed intermediate quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that Zayd's heart and brain can be balanced in opposite ways. Which organ of yours do you think is farthest from its God-given moderation? What would it cost you to bring it back into balance starting today?
?
You are unique: your heart's moderation is not your brain's. In what area of your life have you been forcing a one-size-fits-all 'balance' that actually ignored your personal temperament? What concrete step will you take this week to honor the moderation Allah designed specifically for you?
?
The author says there is 'no intermediate quality that remains while preserving the name'. Are you chasing a perfect, absolute version of yourself that doesn't exist? If you stopped measuring yourself by someone else's standard, what personal imbalance would you finally admit you need to correct for your own soul's health?

Eight Departures from Hypothetical Moderation
Defines hypothetical moderation and enumerates eight departures by single quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that hypothetical moderation in medicine is limited to eight departures, based on the four primary qualities (hot, cold, moist, dry) each being either excessive or deficient.

Core Idea
From hypothetical moderation, there are exactly eight ways to deviate: too hot, too cold, too moist, or too dry.

Having understood personal moderation, the author now defines 'hypothetical moderation' (i'tidal fardī) as the standard considered in medicine. From this hypothetical balance, the simplest classification yields eight departures – each involving one quality being either excessive or deficient. This forms the basis of humoral pathology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining hypothetical moderation
Read the Arabic sentence that begins with 'يعني الاعتدال الفرضي' and its translation. Notice that it is limited to eight types.
The term 'ينحصر في ثمانية' (limited to eight) is the key claim.
2
Identify the four primary qualities
The passage mentions hot, cold, moist, and dry. These are the four qualities that can vary in a temperament.
3
Enumerate the eight departures
Each quality can be either excessive or deficient. For example, 'hotter than it should be' is excess of heat, while 'colder than it should be' is excess of cold. The opposites (less hot, less cold, etc.) complete the eight.
The passage explicitly lists four: hotter, colder, moister, drier. The other four are implied.
4
Understand the medical significance
These eight departures represent the fundamental imbalances in humoral theory. Diagnosing which quality is off helps determine treatment.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'i'tidal (moderation) in medical temperament. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there are only four departures from moderation (just hotter, colder, moister, drier).

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider the four qualities: heat, cold, moisture, dryness. Which one do you tend to overindulge or underutilize in your daily routines (e.g., diet, sleep, environment)? How might that imbalance affect your relationship with your Creator and others? Commit to one concrete adjustment this week.

Twenty-Four Departures by Two Qualities and Al-Katibi's Objection
Enumerates departures with two non-contrary qualities and presents an objection about contradictory qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author enumerates 24 types of departure with two non-contrary qualities, and how al-Katibi objects that contradictory qualities can also both deviate, expanding the possibilities.

Core Idea
Al-Katibi's objection shows that the number of possible departures is far greater than 24.

In this passage, the author continues categorizing departures from moderation, focusing on cases involving two qualities. He initially enumerates 24 types by combining six pairs of non-contrary qualities with four states of increase and decrease. Then al-Katibi raises an important objection: contradictory qualities like heat and cold can also both deviate from the suitable amount, which would generate many more possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the enumeration of 24 types
The author lists six pairs of two non-contrary qualities (heat-cold, heat-moisture, heat-dryness, cold-moisture, cold-dryness, moisture-dryness) and multiplies by four states: both increased, both decreased, first increased second decreased, first decreased second increased. Identify these combinations.
Notice that the initial enumeration assumes the two qualities are not contradictory, i.e., heat and cold are never paired together in this list.
2
Read al-Katibi's objection carefully
Al-Katibi argues that it is possible to have both heat and cold exceed the suitable amount, or both fall short, without one dominating the other. This would add new types beyond the 24.
His key point is that the criterion is deviation from the suitable amount, not dominance over the other quality.
3
Grasp the key distinction
The author's initial scheme considered only non-contrary pairs to avoid simultaneous dominance. But al-Katibi's objection shows that contradictory qualities can both be excessive or deficient relative to the midpoint, which does not require one to dominate the other.
This distinction between 'over the other' and 'over the suitable amount' is crucial.
4
Consider the implications
If contradictory pairs are allowed, then departures can involve one, two, three, or all four qualities. This opens the door to many more types beyond the 24, possibly up to 80 or more depending on the combinatorics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أربعة وعشرون قسماً لأن الكيفيتين الخارجتين، إما الحرارة مع البرودة أو مع الرطوبة أو مع اليبوسة. وإما البرودة مع الرطوبة أو مع اليبوسة ، وإما الرطوبة مع اليبوسة، فهذه ستة نضربها في أربع حالات، هي زيادة الكيفيتين ونقصانهما، وزيادة الأولى مع نقصان الثانية وبالعكس. واعترض الكاتبي في شرح الملخص . بأن الخروج عن هذا الاعتدال بكيفيتين متضادتين ممكن بأن تزيد الحرارة والبرودة جميعاً على القدر اللائق بالممتزج، أو تنقصا عنه، وكذا الرطوبة واليبوسة، ولا يلزم من ذلك كون المتضادتين غالبتين ومغلوبتين معاً في الخارج عن الاعتدال الحقيقي، لأن المعتبر ثمة زيادة كل على الأخرى. وههنا على القدر اللائق ، لا على الأخرى. وإذا جاز ذلك فالخروج إما أن يكون بكيفية، أو كيفيتين أو ثلاث كيفيات أو الكيفيات الأربع جميعاً
And the second: twenty-four types, because the two departing qualities are either heat with cold, or heat with moisture, or heat with dryness, or cold with moisture, or cold with dryness, or moisture with dryness. These are six combinations, multiplied by four states: increase of both qualities, decrease of both, increase of the first with decrease of the second, and vice versa. And al-Katibi objected in his commentary on al-Mulakhkhas that departure from this moderation by two contradictory qualities is possible: heat and cold both exceeding the amount suitable for the mixture, or both falling short of it, and similarly moisture and dryness. And that does not necessitate the two contradictories being dominant and dominated together in departure from true moderation, because what is considered there is the increase of each over the other, while here [consideration is] over the suitable amount, not over the other. And if that is permissible, then departure may be by one quality, or two, or three, or all four qualities.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of departures from medical moderation. The second category involves two qualities, non-contrary only in the initial enumeration. The author lists six combinations of two qualities from the four (hot, cold, moist, dry), such as hot with moist, etc. Each combination has four possible states: both increased, both decreased, first increased and second decreased, and first decreased and second increased, yielding 6 × 4 = 24 types. Then al-Katibi objects: departure by two contradictory qualities (hot-cold, moist-dry) is also possible. He argues that heat and cold can both be excessive or both deficient simultaneously, because the criterion is not dominance of one over the other but deviation from the suitable amount. Thus he opens the door to many more possible departures, including those with three or four qualities.

Key Terms
الخروج بكيفيتين (al-khurūj bi-kayfiyyatayn)
المتضادتان (al-mutaḍāddatān)
القدر اللائق (al-qadr al-lā'iq)
الكاتبي (al-Kātibī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contradictory qualities (like heat and cold) cannot both be excessive or deficient simultaneously in a mixture.
Correction: Al-Katibi explains that they can both exceed or fall short of the suitable amount, because the measure is not dominance over each other but deviation from the midpoint.

Checkpoint

A) 12

C) 24
D) 36

Key Takeaway
Al-Katibi's objection reveals that if the criterion is deviation from the suitable amount rather than dominance, contradictory qualities can both deviate, vastly expanding the possible types of departure from moderation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Katibi questioned an accepted classification because he saw the criterion was flawed. Is there a belief or practice in your life that you have accepted without examining the criterion behind it? What would it take to scrutinize it this week?
?
The passage contrasts measuring by dominance over another versus measuring by suitability to a standard. In your spiritual life, do you measure yourself by comparing to others, or by how well you align with what Allah requires of you personally? Which standard will you consciously adopt today?
?
Al-Katibi's objection opened up many more possibilities. Are you open to having your understanding of Islamic teachings challenged and expanded, or do you prefer a closed system for comfort? What might you lose or gain by being truly open to deeper scrutiny?

Completing the 80 Types: Proportions and Moderation in Mixtures
Completes enumeration to 80 types and presents al-Shirazi's proportional definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shirazi completed the enumeration of departures from moderation to 80 types and his proportional definition of moderation.

Core Idea
Moderation is about proportion, not fixed quantities — a shift in scale preserves balance.

This passage concludes the exhaustive enumeration of all possible departures from the balanced mixture, reaching a total of 80 types. It then presents al-Shirazi's crucial insight that moderation is defined by the preservation of proportional ratios, not absolute magnitudes. This resolves apparent contradictions in earlier classifications.

Guided Path
1
Trace the calculation of the third category
See how the four combinations of three qualities multiplied by eight states yield 32 types. Follow the multiplication carefully.
The eight states are: increase all, decrease all, increase two and decrease one, etc.
2
Understand the fourth category
The sixteen types come from increase/decrease of all four, increase one decrease three, increase two decrease two (six subtypes). Verify the arithmetic.
The two can be active or passive or mixed.
3
Note the objector's error and the total
Al-Katibi missed some divisions, arriving at 63. Al-Shirazi completed to 80. Add the previous categories (8+24+32+16=80).
4
Grasp al-Shirazi's answer on moderation
Moderation means preserving the proportion (nisba) that best suits the mixture's actions. The example with heat double cold illustrates that scaling both up or down keeps moderation.
Only when the ratio is disturbed is there a true departure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث اثنان وثلاثون قسماً، لأن الخروج، إما بالحرارة مع البرودة والرطوبة، أو مع البرودة واليبوسة، أو مع الرطوبة واليبوسة، وإما بالبرودة مع الرطوبة واليبوسة يصير أربعة نضربها في ثمان حالات هي زيادة الكيفيات الثلاث، ونقصانها، وزيادة كل من نقصان الأخريين، ونقصان كل مع زيادة الأخريين. والرابع ستة عشر قسماً على عدد الحالات الممكنة. أعني زيادة الكيفيات الأربع ونقصانها، وزيادة كل منها مع نقصان الثلاث الباقية .وبالعكس. فهذه عشرة، وزيادة كل اثنتين مع نقصان الآخريين وهذه ستة . لأن الاثنتين إما الفاعلتان وإما المنفعلتان، وإما كل من الفاعلتين مع كل من المنفعلتين. والمعترض قد أخل ببعض هذه الأقسام، فجعل الأقسام الممكنة ثلاثة وستين، فاستوفاها العلامة الشيرازي ثمانين . ثم أجاب : بأن معنى هذا الاعتدال هو أن يتوفر على الممتزج من كميات العناصر وكيفياتها القسط الذي هو أليق بحاله وأنسب بأفعاله. أعني أن تكون الحرارة والبرودة فيه على نسبة تلائم أفعاله على الوجه الأفضل الأليق، وكذا الرطوبة واليبوسة، فما دامت هذه النسبة محفوظة ، كان الاعتدال باقياً، وإن فرض زيادة أو نقصان في مقادير الكيفيات مثلاً، إذا كان اللائق بالممتزج أن يكون الحار ضعف البارد كأن يكون الحار من عشرة إلى عشرين والبارد من خمسة إلى عشرة ، فإذا زادت الفاعلتان، فصارت الحرارة اثنتي عشرة والبرودة ستة ، أو انتقصتا فصارت الحرارة ثمانية والبرودة أربعة فإن الاعتدال باق لبقاء النسبة. وإن صارت البرودة ستة مع كون الحرارة أحد عشر فليس هذا خروجا عن الاعتدال بالكيفيتين، بل بالبرودة فقط، إذ المزاج صار أبرد مما ينبغي لا أحر،
And the third: thirty-two types, because departure is either by heat with cold and moisture, or with cold and dryness, or with moisture and dryness, or by cold with moisture and dryness — these become four, multiplied by eight states: increase of the three qualities, decrease of them, increase of each and decrease of the other two, and decrease of each with increase of the other two. And the fourth: sixteen types according to the number of possible states: increase of the four qualities and decrease of them, increase of each with decrease of the remaining three, and vice versa — these are ten, and increase of each two with decrease of the other two — these are six, because the two are either the active [qualities] or the passive [qualities], or each of the active with each of the passive. And the objector had omitted some of these divisions, making the possible divisions sixty-three, but the scholar al-Shirazi completed them to eighty. Then he answered: that the meaning of this moderation is that the portion of the quantities and qualities of the elements that is most suitable for its state and most appropriate for its actions is bestowed upon the mixture. That is, the heat and cold therein are in a proportion agreeable to its actions in the best and most suitable manner, and likewise moisture and dryness. As long as this proportion is preserved, moderation remains, even if an increase or decrease in the magnitudes of the qualities is assumed. For example, if it is suitable for the mixture that the hot be double the cold, such that the hot is from ten to twenty and the cold from five to ten, then if the active [qualities] increase, so heat becomes twelve and cold six, or they decrease so heat becomes eight and cold four, moderation remains because the proportion is preserved. But if cold becomes six while heat is eleven, this is not a departure from moderation by two qualities, but only by cold, for the temperament became colder than it should be, not hotter.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage completes the enumeration of departures from moderation and provides the definitive answer by al-Shirazi. The third category: three qualities, with four combinations of three qualities from the four, multiplied by eight possible states (increase all, decrease all, increase two and decrease one, etc.) = 32 types. The fourth category: all four qualities, with sixteen possible states (increase all, decrease all, increase one and decrease three, increase two and decrease two, etc.) = 16 types. Adding these to the previous 8 and 24 gives a total of 80 types. The objector (al-Katibi) had arrived at 63, but al-Shirazi completed it to 80. Then al-Shirazi's answer explains that true moderation (i'tidal) means the preservation of a proportional ratio (nisba) between the qualities relative to what best suits the mixture's actions. The example illustrates: if the suitable ratio is heat twice cold, then scaling both up or down while maintaining that ratio keeps moderation. Only when the ratio is disturbed does a real departure occur. This resolves the confusion between absolute quantities and proportional balance.

Key Terms
الاعتدال (al-iʿtidāl)
النسبة (al-nisba)
الفاعلتان (al-fāʿilatān)
المنفعلتان (al-munfaʿilatān)
الشيرازي (al-Shīrāzī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moderation requires fixed absolute quantities of qualities.
Correction: Al-Shirazi clarifies that moderation is about proportional balance; quantities can change as long as the ratio remains.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shirazi, when does a mixture remain in moderation despite changes in quantities?
A) When the quantities remain exactly the same
B) When the proportion between qualities is preserved
C) When there is no change in any quality
D) When the active qualities dominate

Key Takeaway
There are 80 possible types of departure from moderation, and moderation itself is defined by the preservation of a proportional ratio suitable to the mixture's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one area in your life where you treat balance as a fixed quantity—like a certain amount of worship, work, or rest you do every day regardless of circumstances. According to this lesson, balance is about proportion suited to your state. Is there a hidden imbalance in your routine because you're clinging to a quantity that no longer fits your purpose? Will you examine that area today and recalibrate?
?
Al-Shirazi says moderation is about what is 'most suitable for its actions.' Your soul has actions and purposes unique to you. Are there areas—like your anger, your contentment, your striving—where you think you are 'balanced' because you feel moderate, but actually a real disproportion exists that you haven't noticed? What would it cost you to ignore that slight disproportion?
?
You just learned that a single quality can be off—like the mixture being 'colder than it should be'—while other qualities seem fine. Is there one quality in your character or devotion (e.g., fear, hope, patience, urgency) that is disproportionate even though you feel everything else is normal? If you don't restore the proportion, what gradual damage might it cause? What step will you take this week to correct it?

Moderation in Mixtures: Proportional Balance and Refutation
Al-Taftazani defines moderation and refutes an objection on enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that moderation (al-i'tidal) in mixtures is defined by maintaining a proportion of qualities suitable for the mixture's actions, not by equal quantities, and how an objection regarding the enumeration of possible states of imbalance is refuted.

Core Idea
Moderation is about preserving the right proportion, not equal amounts.

In this passage, al-Taftazani clarifies the concept of moderation in mixtures by distinguishing it from simple equality. He then addresses a mathematical objection about the number of possible departures from moderation, showing that the objector's enumeration was incomplete and that the correct understanding of moderation as proportional eliminates the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claimed there are 63 possible states of departure from moderation based on combinations of qualities. Read the opening enumeration to see how he arrived at this number.
The enumeration involves the three qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) and their increases/decreases.
2
Understand the definition of moderation
Al-Taftazani answers that moderation is not about equal quantities but about preserving the proportion of qualities that best suits the mixture's actions. Read the definition carefully.
The key phrase: 'ما دامت هذه النسبة محفوظة، كان الاعتدال باقياً'
3
Work through the example
Consider the proportion where heat is double cold. If the ratio 2:1 is maintained (e.g., 12:6 or 8:4), moderation remains. A change in only one quality (e.g., 11:6) causes departure in that quality alone.
This shows that moderation can persist despite changes in absolute quantities.
4
See the refutation
Because the proportion is fixed, simultaneous departure in both qualities (hot and cold) is impossible in the way the objector assumed. A change in one direction precludes the opposite change in the other.
This undermines the objector's claim that both can depart together.
5
Note the exception for individual temperaments
The author admits that for specific organs, departure in both opposite qualities is possible because there is no single intermediate quality for the whole body.
This nuance shows the depth of the analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثلاث مع . وثلاثون قسماً، لأن الخروج، إما بالحرارة مع البرودة والرطوبة، أو مع البرودة واليبوسة، أي مع الرطوبة واليبوسة، وإما بالبرودة مع الرطوبة واليبوسة يصير أربعة نضربها في ثمان حالات هي زيادة الكيفيات الثلاث، ونقصانها، وزيادة كل من نقصان الأخريين، ونقصان كل مع زيادة الأخريين. والرابع ستة عشر قسماً على عدد الحالات الممكنة. أعني زيادة الكيفيات الأربع ونقصانها، وزيادة كل منها مع نقصان الثلاث الباقية .وبالعكس. فهذه عشرة، وزيادة كل اثنتين مع نقصان الآخريين وهذه ستة . لأن الاثنتين إما الفاعلتان وإما المنفعلتان، وإما كل من الفاعلتين مع كل من المنفعلتين. والمعترض قد أخل ببعض هذه الأقسام، فجعل الأقسام الممكنة ثلاثة وستين، فاستوفاها العلامة الشيرازي ثمانين . ثم أجاب : بأن معنى هذا الاعتدال هو أن يتوفر على الممتزج من كميات العناصر وكيفياتها القسط الذي هو أليق بحاله وأنسب بأفعاله. أعني أن تكون الحرارة والبرودة فيه على نسبة تلائم أفعاله على الوجه الأفضل الأليق، وكذا الرطوبة واليبوسة، فما دامت هذه النسبة محفوظة ، كان الاعتدال باقياً، وإن فرض زيادة أو نقصان في مقادير الكيفيات مثلاً، إذا كان اللائق بالممتزج أن يكون الحار ضعف البارد كأن يكون الحار من عشرة إلى عشرين والبارد من خمسة إلى عشرة ، فإذا زادت الفاعلتان، فصارت الحرارة اثنتي عشرة والبرودة ستة ، أو انتقصتا فصارت الحرارة ثمانية والبرودة أربعة فإن الاعتدال باق لبقاء النسبة. وإن صارت البرودة ستة مع كون الحرارة أحد عشر فليس هذا خروجا عن الاعتدال بالكيفيتين، بل بالبرودة فقط، إذ المزاج صار أبرد مما ينبغي لا أحر، ومع كون الحرارة ثلاثة عشر فليس إلا خروجاً عن الاعتدال بالحرارة، حيث صار أحر مما ينبغي، وكذا في الرطوبة واليبوسة والحاصل أنه إذا كانت النسبة الفاصلة بأن تكون الخرارة ضعف البرودة مثلاً، فتغير النسبة إما أن يكون بزيادة الحرارة على الضعف أو نقصانها أو زيادة البرودة على النصف أو نقصانها ولا يتصور مع زيادة الحرارة على الضعف نقصان البرودة عن الضعف ولا مع نقصانها عن الضعف زيادة البرودة على النصف، وكذا الكلام في كميات العناصر، فلا يرد ههنا ما يرد عن الاعتدال الحقيقي من أنه لما اعتبر فيه تساوي العناصر في الكم أيضاً، جاز الخروج عنه بالعنصر الحار والبارد جميعاً، بأن يزيد آخر ما على الآخرين، وذلك لأن المعتبر ههنا نسبة بين كميات العناصر كالضعف والنصف مثلاً، فتغير النسبة لا يتصور إلا مثل ما ذكر في الكيفيتين فليتأمل . نعم لا يبعد الخروج بالكيفيتين المتضادتين عن الاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الداخل، بأن يصير مزاج قلب زيد أحر وأيبس من أعدل أحواله، ومزاج دماغه أبرد وأرطب أو بالعكس وذلك لأنه ليس هناك كيفية متوسطة يحكم ببقائها ما دامت النسبة محفوظة وإن كانت المقادير مختلفة .
The three and thirty sections, because departure [from moderation] is either by heat with cold and moisture, or with cold and dryness, i.e., with moisture and dryness, or by cold with moisture and dryness, yielding four [cases] which we multiply by eight states which are the increase of the three qualities, their decrease, the increase of each along with the decrease of the other two, and the decrease of each along with the increase of the other two. The fourth [category] is sixteen sections according to the number of possible states: namely, the increase of the four qualities and their decrease, and the increase of each of them along with the decrease of the remaining three, and vice versa. So these are ten, and the increase of each two along with the decrease of the other two, and these are six, because the two are either the active [qualities] or the passive [qualities], or each of the active along with each of the passive. The objector has omitted some of these divisions, making the possible sections sixty-three, but the distinguished al-Shirazi completed them as eighty. Then he answered: The meaning of this moderation is that the mixture receives from the quantities of the elements and their qualities the portion that is most suitable for its state and most appropriate for its actions. I mean that the heat and cold in it are in a proportion that suits its actions in the best and most fitting manner, and likewise moisture and dryness. As long as this proportion is preserved, moderation remains, even if an increase or decrease in the measures of the qualities is assumed. For example, if it is suitable for the mixture that the hot be twice the cold, such that the hot is from ten to twenty and the cold from five to ten, then if the two active qualities increase, so that heat becomes twelve and cold six, or they decrease so that heat becomes eight and cold four, then moderation remains due to the preservation of the proportion. If cold becomes six while heat is eleven, this is not a departure from moderation in both qualities, but only in cold, because the temperament became colder than it should be, not hotter. And if heat becomes thirteen, it is only a departure from moderation in heat, since it became hotter than it should be. Similarly for moisture and dryness. The conclusion is that when the distinguishing proportion is, for example, that heat is twice cold, then the change in proportion is either by an increase of heat beyond double, or its decrease, or an increase of cold beyond half, or its decrease. And it is inconceivable that with an increase of heat beyond double there be a decrease of cold below double, nor with a decrease of cold below double an increase of cold beyond half. The same applies to the quantities of the elements. So the objection that applies to real moderation—that since equality of the elements in quantity is also considered, departure from it can occur in both the hot and the cold element simultaneously, by one exceeding the others—does not apply here, because what is considered here is a proportion between the quantities of the elements, such as double and half, so a change in proportion can only be conceived as mentioned for the two qualities, so let this be contemplated. Indeed, it is not far-fetched that there be a departure in both opposite qualities from the individual moderation relative to the interior, such that the temperament of Zayd's heart becomes hotter and drier than its most balanced state, and the temperament of his brain becomes colder and more moist, or vice versa, because there is no intermediate quality that can be judged to persist as long as the proportion is preserved, even if the quantities differ.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of moderation (al-i'tidal) in mixtures and refutes an objection concerning the enumeration of possible states of imbalance. The author begins by presenting a detailed enumeration of possible cases of departure from moderation based on the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) and their interactions. He explains that when considering the three qualities (presumably heat, cold, and one other) and their increases and decreases, there are specific numbers of possible states. Multiplying these yields 33, and further combinations lead to 63, which the objector claimed. However, al-Shirazi completed the count to 80 by including all possible combinations. The author then gives the answer: moderation is not defined by equal amounts, but by the proportion most suitable for the mixture's actions. He illustrates with an example where the suitable proportion is that heat is twice cold. If the proportion is maintained (e.g., 12:6 or 8:4), moderation remains. If only one quality changes disproportionately (e.g., cold becomes 6 while heat is 11), there is departure in that quality alone. He then explains that when the proportion is fixed (e.g., double), changes can only occur in limited ways, and it is impossible for both qualities to change in opposite directions simultaneously while preserving the ratio. This refutes the objection that both hot and cold could depart together. Finally, he notes that departure in both opposite qualities is possible in individual temperaments when there is no single intermediate quality, as with different organs having different ideal proportions.

Key Terms
اعتدال
مزاج
كيفيات
عناصر
نسبة
ممتزج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moderation in mixtures requires that the quantities of the four qualities be exactly equal.
Correction: Moderation is defined by preserving the proportion of qualities that best suits the mixture's actions, not equal quantities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for the continuation of moderation in a mixture?
A) The quantities of the four qualities being exactly equal.
B) The preservation of the proportion of qualities that suits the mixture's actions.
C) The absence of any increase or decrease in the qualities.
D) The mixture being at the equator.

Key Takeaway
Moderation in mixtures depends on preserving the proportion of qualities that best suits the mixture's functions, not on having equal amounts, and this proportional understanding resolves apparent difficulties in enumerating possible imbalances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring situation where you struggle to maintain balance—whether in emotions, speech, or action. Are you aiming for a 'perfect equal balance' or for the right proportion that suits that specific context, as this lesson teaches? How might you be misapplying the concept of moderation by ignoring the relativity to function? What would it look like to seek the proportion that is 'most suitable for your state'? Write down one concrete adjustment you will make this week.
?
In the example, the ideal proportion is 2:1 heat to cold. What if your own life's 'ideal proportion' for a particular virtue (like patience vs. urgency, or mercy vs. justice) is not 50/50 but heavily weighted one way depending on the situation? This lesson says moderation is not equal measures but the proportion that best serves the action. Where have you been rigidly clinging to equal distribution out of fear of extremism, while missing the true moderation of fit? Pause and name one area where you need to recalibrate based on purpose, not on numbers.
?
Al-Taftazani refutes the objector by showing that a misunderstanding of moderation leads to false conclusions about possible imbalances. Consider a belief you hold about your own spiritual state—perhaps you think you are 'balanced' because you do a little of everything. Could your assumption be like the objector's, based on an incomplete enumeration of your inner qualities? What would it take to honestly examine whether the proportion of your heart's qualities is actually suitable for your actions in the sight of Allah? Commit to one hour of self-accountability (muhasaba) this week using this proportional lens.

The Logic of Imbalance in Qualities and Ratios
The author explains the logic of ratio change leading to imbalance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imbalance in the four primary qualities results from changes in their ratios, and how this principle applies to both general temperament and personal organs.

Core Idea
Imbalance is a change in ratio, not just excess.

This passage from al-Maqasid clarifies a subtle point about the four primary qualities. The author explains that imbalance is not simply an increase or decrease in a quality, but a shift in the specific ratio between opposing qualities. He then applies this to the four elements and to individual organs. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the logic of temperament theory.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline ratio
The author uses the example of heat being double cold. So the ratio is 2:1. This is the 'distinguishing ratio' that defines the balanced state.
The baseline ratio can be any specific proportion; double/half is just an example.
2
Identify possible changes
The change in ratio can occur in four ways: increase of heat beyond double, decrease of heat below double, increase of cold beyond half, or decrease of cold below half.
3
Notice the constraints
The author explains that some combinations are impossible: e.g., heat increasing beyond double while cold decreases from double, or heat decreasing from double while cold increases beyond half.
This is because both qualities cannot simultaneously shift in the same direction.
4
Apply to elements
The same principle holds for the quantities of the four elements. The ratio between element quantities, not absolute equality, determines balance.
This answers a potential objection about true moderation.
5
Consider the exception for personal organs
In specific organs like the heart or brain, it is possible to depart from personal moderation by two opposite qualities (e.g., heart hotter and drier, brain colder and moister) because there is no intermediate quality preserved.
This nuance shows that ratio preservation can occur with varying absolute magnitudes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحرارة ثلاثة عشر فليس إلا خروجاً عن الاعتدال بالحرارة، حيث صار أحر مما ينبغي، وكذا في الرطوبة واليبوسة والحاصل أنه إذا كانت النسبة الفاصلة بأن تكون الحرارة ضعف البرودة مثلاً، فتغير النسبة إما أن يكون بزيادة الحرارة على الضعف أو نقصانها أو زيادة البرودة على النصف أو نقصانها ولا يتصور مع زيادة الحرارة على الضعف نقصان البرودة عن الضعف ولا مع نقصانها عن الضعف زيادة البرودة على النصف، وكذا الكلام في كميات العناصر، فلا يرد ههنا ما يرد عن الاعتدال الحقيقي من أنه لما اعتبر فيه تساوي العناصر في الكم أيضاً، جاز الخروج عنه بالعنصر الحار والبارد جميعاً، بأن يزيد آخر ما على الآخرين، وذلك لأن المعتبر ههنا نسبة بين كميات العناصر كالضعف والنصف مثلاً، فتغير النسبة لا يتصور إلا مثل ما ذكر في الكيفيتين فليتأمل . نعم لا يبعد الخروج بالكيفيتين المتضادتين عن الاعتدال الشخصي بالنسبة إلى الداخل، بأن يصير مزاج قلب زيد أحر وأيبس من أعدل أحواله، ومزاج دماغه أبرد وأرطب أو بالعكس وذلك لأنه ليس هناك كيفية متوسطة يحكم ببقائها ما دامت النسبة محفوظة وإن كانت المقادير مختلفة .
Heat is thirteen? [unclear] it is nothing but departure from moderation in heat, where it becomes hotter than it should, and likewise in moisture and dryness. The conclusion is that if the distinguishing ratio is that heat is double cold, for example, then the change in the ratio is either by an increase of heat over the double, or a decrease of it, or an increase of cold over the half, or a decrease of it. And it is inconceivable with increase of heat over the double that there be a decrease of cold from the double, nor with decrease of heat from the double an increase of cold over the half. And similarly for the quantities of the elements. So what is objected concerning true moderation—that since equality of the elements in quantity is also considered in it, departure from it by both the hot and cold element is possible, such that one exceeds the other two—does not apply here, because what is considered here is a ratio between the quantities of the elements, such as double and half. Thus a change in the ratio is inconceivable except as mentioned in the two qualities, so reflect. Yes, it is not unlikely to depart from personal moderation with respect to internal [organs] by two opposite qualities, such that the temperament of Zayd's heart becomes hotter and drier than its most balanced state, and the temperament of his brain becomes colder and moister, or the opposite. And that is because there is no intermediate quality that is judged to remain as long as the ratio is preserved, even if the quantities differ.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of imbalance in the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) and how it relates to the ratio between them. The author begins by stating that heat being thirteen [perhaps a reference to a scale] is simply a departure from moderation. He then explains that when there is a distinguishing ratio (e.g., heat being double cold), any change in that ratio must occur by specific increases or decreases: either heat increases beyond double, heat decreases below double, cold increases beyond half, or cold decreases below half. It is impossible for heat to increase beyond double while cold simultaneously decreases from double, or for heat to decrease from double while cold increases from half. This principle applies similarly to the quantities of the elements (earth, water, air, fire). The author then responds to a potential objection: one might think that since true moderation involves equal quantities of elements, a departure could occur by both hot and cold elements exceeding the others. However, he clarifies that here the relevant factor is the ratio between quantities (like double and half), not absolute equality. Consequently, the only possible changes are those previously described. The second paragraph presents an exception: it is possible to depart from personal moderation in specific organs (like the heart or brain) by two opposite qualities (hotter and drier, or colder and moister), because there is no single intermediate quality that remains constant. Instead, the ratio is preserved even if the absolute quantities differ. This nuance guards against oversimplifying the concept of moderation in complex mixtures.

Key Terms
اعتدال
نسبة
كيفيات
عناصر
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any increase in a quality leads to imbalance.
Correction: The author shows that imbalance specifically involves a change in a set ratio, not mere excess. For example, heat increasing beyond double is imbalance, but heat increasing while cold decreases from double is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if heat is double cold and the ratio changes, which of the following is possible?
A) Increase of heat over double with decrease of cold from double
B) Increase of heat over double with increase of cold over half
C) Increase of cold over half with heat remaining at double
D) Decrease of heat from double with increase of cold over half

Key Takeaway
True imbalance is a change in the prescribed ratio between opposing qualities, not simply an excess or deficiency of one quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life have you assumed that 'more' of a good thing automatically means balance, ignoring the ratio? For example, more work without intentional rest, or more worship without attention to your duties. How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The author shows that imbalance is a shift in a set ratio, not just excess or deficiency. Think of a relationship that feels off. Have you been focusing only on one side of the equation? What specific 'ratio' might need restoration?
?
If your spiritual life had a 'distinguishing ratio'—like between hope of mercy and fear of punishment—can you honestly say you're maintaining it? What one change would bring it back to equilibrium, and what will you do about it today?

The Most Balanced Region: Equator vs Fourth Clime
Debate on the most balanced region with arguments and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over which region is most balanced and why the majority favored the fourth clime over the equator.

Core Idea
True climatic balance is more than equal day and night; it involves temperament and perfection.

The author presents a dispute among scholars about the most balanced region on Earth based on celestial positions. Ibn Sina argues for the equator, but the majority prefers the fourth clime for its observed perfections. This lesson examines the arguments and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the debate. Notice the flow: Ibn Sina's arguments, then the author's refutation, then the majority view.
The Arabic text cuts off mid-sentence due to manuscript issues; the main argument is complete.
2
Analyze Ibn Sina's reasoning
Identify each of Ibn Sina's points: similarity of conditions, rapid zenith passing, day-night equality, and habituation to heat. Ask yourself why he thinks these support balance.
3
Understand the refutation
The author responds that similarity (lack of change) does not equal true moderation. Reflect on what 'true moderation' might mean beyond mere constancy.
The refutation is a key logical move.
4
Learn the majority view
The majority favors the fourth clime based on observable perfections (civilization, science, etc.) that depend on a balanced temperament. Consider why this empirical argument might be stronger.
The passage ends here; the reasoning continues in the original text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في أعدل البقاع بحسب أوضاع العلويات : فقال ابن سينا . خط الاستواء لتشابه أحوالهم في البرد والحر، ولا يضر كونهم دائماً في المسامتة أو القرب منها، لأنها لسرعة زوالها قليلة النكاية، ولا كون شتائهم مثل صيف البلدة التي عرضها ضعف الميل الكلي في مسامتة الشمس مع أنه في غاية الحر لجواز أن يكون ذلك لتزايد نهاره على الليل إلى قريب من الضعف، بخلاف خط الاستواء فإنهما يتساويان فيه أبداً، فيتعادل البرد والحر، أو يكون أهل خط الاستواء لما ألفوا بالحر لم تتأثر أمزجتهم عن حر المسامتة، واستبردوا الهواء حين الشمس في المنقلب فلم ينحرفوا عن الاعتدال . والجواب : أن التشابه بمعنى عدم طريان تغير معتد به لا يفيد القرب من الاعتدال الحقيقي على ما هو المتنازع . وقال الأكثرون هو الإقليم الرابع لما يشاهد فيه من زيادة الكمالات التابعة للمزاج، الذي كلما كان إلى الاعتدال
And they differed concerning the most balanced of regions according to the positions of the celestial bodies. Ibn Sina said: The equator, because of the similarity of their conditions in cold and heat. And it does not harm that they are always in the zenith or near it, because due to its rapid passing it has little effect. Nor does it harm that their winter is like the summer of a town whose latitude is double the maximum declination in the zenith of the sun, although it is extremely hot, because it is possible that this is due to the increase of its day over the night to nearly double, unlike the equator, for they are always equal there, so cold and heat are balanced. Or it may be that the people of the equator, due to their habituation to heat, their temperaments are not affected by the heat of the zenith, and they find the air cool when the sun is in the tropic, so they do not deviate from moderation. The answer: similarity in the sense of the absence of a significant change does not bring one near to true moderation, which is the point of contention. And the majority said: It is the fourth clime, because of what is observed in it of increased perfections that depend on temperament, which the more it tends toward moderation
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a debate about which geographic region on Earth has the most balanced (i.e., moderate) climate, which affects the temperament of its inhabitants. The author begins by stating that scholars differed based on celestial positions. Ibn Sina argued for the equator, citing several reasons: the similarity of conditions in cold and heat; the fact that the sun passes quickly overhead, so its effect is minimal; that even if winter is like a hot summer elsewhere, the day length variation cancels out; and that people become habituated to heat. The author then gives an answer that refutes Ibn Sina's argument: mere similarity (lack of significant change) does not constitute true moderation, which is the criterion under dispute. The majority opinion favors the fourth clime, based on observable perfections (such as civilization and intellectual advancement) that depend on a balanced temperament. The implication is that the fourth clime is closer to the ideal balance. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that further explanation follows in the original text.

Key Terms
أعدل البقاع
خط الاستواء
الإقليم الرابع
اعتدال
مزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The equator is automatically the most balanced region because it has equal day and night.
Correction: The author argues that true moderation involves more than just lack of variation; similarity in climate does not mean a balanced temperament fosters perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the majority opinion in this passage, which region is considered the most balanced?
A) The equator
B) The fourth clime
C) The polar regions
D) The region with the longest day

Key Takeaway
The majority opinion favors the fourth clime as the most balanced region because it exhibits greater perfections dependent on a balanced temperament, not just climatic constancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard that true balance is more than just a lack of upheaval. In your own life, do you settle for a comfortable but stagnant routine (like the 'similarity' of the equator), or do you actively seek conditions that produce growth and perfection? What specific change can you make this week to move from mere stability to true moderation?
?
Ibn Sina argued that people at the equator become habituated to heat and don't feel its effects. Are there spiritual 'heat' or trials in your life that you have become so used to that you no longer feel their intensity? Could your 'normal' be keeping you from a more balanced state that God intends for you?
?
The majority pointed to observed perfections—civilization and intellect—as evidence of a balanced region. Look at your own habits and environment: are they producing visible fruits of wisdom, patience, and purpose? If not, what is the one factor you can adjust to tilt your 'climate' toward true perfection?

Definition of al-Iqlim and the Seven Climes
Defines al-iqlim and describes the seven climes with dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-iqlim and the arrangement of the seven climes in the inhabited quarter of the earth.

Core Idea
The seven climes are geographic divisions varying in size, with the first largest and seventh smallest.

This lesson introduces the geographic concept of al-iqlim (clime) as used in classical Islamic cosmology. The author defines it as a named section of the earth and describes the seven climes that make up the inhabited quarter. Understanding this framework is necessary for later discussions about the most balanced clime and its relation to divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's description of the climes come through clearly.
2
Identify the definition and number
Find how al-iqlim is defined and how many climes are said to exist in the inhabited quarter.
The passage states there are seven climes.
3
Compare the first and seventh climes
Note the dimensions given for the first clime (largest) and the seventh clime (smallest). Compare their lengths and widths.
This will help you understand the range of sizes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأقليم : قسم من الأرض يختص باسم ، والأقاليم سبعة أقسام في الربع المسكون من الأرض كل أقليم منها كأنه بساط مفروش قد مدطوله من المشرق إلى المغرب، وعرضه من الجنوب إلى الشمال مختلفة الطول والعرض فأطولها وأعرضها الأقليم الأول، وذلك أن طوله من المشرق إلى المغرب نحو ثلاثة آلاف فرسخ ، وعرضه من الجنوب إلى الشمال نحو مئة وخمسين فرسخاً وأقصرها طولاً وعرضاً الأقليم السابع ، وذلك أن طوله من المشرق إلى المغرب نحو من ألف وخمسمائة فرسخ ، وعرضه من الجنوب إلى الشمال نحو من سبعين فرسخاً وأما سائر الأقاليم ففيما بينهما من الطول والعرض، وهذا مثال ذلك.
Al-iqlim: a section of the earth distinguished by a name. The climes are seven divisions in the inhabited quarter of the earth; each clime is like a spread carpet, its length from east to west and its breadth from south to north, varying in length and width. The longest and widest is the first clime, its length from east to west about three thousand farsakhs, and its breadth from south to north about one hundred and fifty farsakhs. The shortest in length and width is the seventh clime, its length from east to west about one thousand five hundred farsakhs, and its breadth from south to north about seventy farsakhs. As for the remaining climes, they are between these in length and width. This is an example of that.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-iqlim' (clime) as a specific geographic division of the earth. The author explains that there are seven climes in the inhabited quarter of the earth. Each clime is described as a carpet-like region extending from east to west (length) and south to north (breadth). The first clime is the largest, with a length of about 3,000 farsakhs and a width of about 150 farsakhs. The seventh clime is the smallest, with a length of about 1,500 farsakhs and a width of about 70 farsakhs. The other climes fall between these extremes in size. The passage ends by noting that this is just an illustrative example. This geographic framework serves as background for the theological argument about which clime has the most balanced temperament, a topic relevant to divine wisdom in creation.

Key Terms
إقليم
أقاليم
فرسخ
ربع مسكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many climes (aqalim) are there in the inhabited quarter of the earth according to this passage?
A) Five
B) Seven
C) Four
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The inhabited quarter of the earth is divided into seven climes, each with different dimensions, the first being the largest and the seventh the smallest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the earth spread out like a carpet into these seven climes, each with its precise measurements, does it shift how you see your own place in the world? In what way does that precision in creation call you to trust the Creator more deeply in your daily life?
?
The first clime is the largest and the seventh the smallest. If you were to live in a smaller clime, would you feel less cared for? What does the existence of different sizes teach you about your own sense of worth being anchored in Allah's provision rather than in physical circumstances?

The First Objection: Constant Zenith Proximity and Its Refutation
Taftazani refutes the first objection about equatorial heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sun's brief zenith passage at the equator causes less heating than prolonged near-zenith exposure at higher latitudes.

Core Idea
A permanent weak cause can outweigh a brief strong cause.

The first objection claims that equatorial regions are always under the sun, so they must be hottest. But Taftazani's answer reveals a key principle: the duration of exposure matters more than peak intensity. This refutation introduces the example of iron heated in a gentle fire for a long time versus a strong fire for a short time.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection and its answer. Notice how the objection assumes constant heat leads to maximum temperature.
The objection is from an unnamed critic; Taftazani presents it fairly.
2
Identify the key principle
The answer compares a permanent weak cause to a non-permanent strong cause. Understand why permanence can compensate for weakness.
Think of the iron analogy.
3
Apply to geography
Connect the principle to real geography: the equator has a quick zenith passage, while higher latitudes have longer periods of near-zenith sun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دائماً في المسامتة أو في القرب منها ، فتكون حرارتهم مفرطة، لأن قرب المسامتة في زمان يسير كما في الصيف عندنا ، مع تقدم برد الشتاء المخرج للهواء عن استعداد التسخن مسخن جداً فهذا أولى.
وجوابه : أن مسامتتهم لسرعة زوالها أقل نكاية وتسخيناً للهواء من المسامتة، أو القرب منها في البلاد ذوات العروض، لأن قرب المسامتة يبقى هناك أياماً كثيرة، ويكون النهار أطول من الليل طولاً ظاهراً. والسبب الدائم وإن ضعف، قد يكون أكثر تأثيراً من غير الدائم، وإن قوى كالحديد في نار لينة مدة، وفي نار قوية لحظة . وثانيهما أن زمان وصول الشمس إلى أول السرطان شتاء لخط الاستواء لكون الشمس على غاية البعد عن سمت رأسهم، وصيف البقعة عرضها سبعة وأربعون ضعف الميل الكلي كبلدة سراي لكونها على غاية القرب عنها مع أن بعدها عن سمت رأس البقعتين على السواء، فيكون حر شتاء خط الاستواء كحرّ صيف هذه البلدة، بل أكثر إذا تأملت لأن ما قبل هذه الحالة لهم من أسباب السخونة، ولأهل البلدة من أسباب البرودة، وإذا كان حر شتائهم هذا، فما ظنك بحر صيفهم .
وجوابه : منع تشابه حر الفصلين في البقعتين، وإنما يلزم لو انحصر سبب الحرّ في قرب الشمس من سمت الرأس وهو محال، فيجوز أن يشتد حرصيف البلدة المفروضة، بسبب تزايد طول النهار على الليل إلى الضعف تقريباً، لأن طول نهارها يبلغ ست عشرة ساعة تقريباً، وقصر لياليها ثماني ساعات كذلك، بخلاف خط الاستواء، فإن الليل والنهار فيه دائماً على السواء، فيتعادل الحر والبرد، وأيضاً المألوف لا يؤثر ، فلعل أهل خط الاستواء لإلفهم بالحر لا تتأثر أمزجتهم ، ولا تتسخن من حر مسامتة الشمس، ويستبردون الهواء عند بعد المسامتة ، أعني كون الشمس في الانقلابين، فيبقى الاعتدال بخلاف البلدة المفروضة، فإن الحر يشتد على جسمهم، ويؤثر فيهم لعدم الفهم به ، ولا نتقالهم إليه من شدة البرد، وإن كان على التدريج، ولا يخفي على المصنف ضعف هذا الجواب، وكذا إسناد حرّ البلدة إلى الأسباب الأرضية .
Always in zenith proximity or near it, so their heat is excessive, because proximity to the zenith in a short time as in our summer, preceded by winter cold that removes the air's readiness to heat, becomes very hot, so this is more likely. Its answer: Their zenith proximity due to its quick passing is less harmful and heating to the air than the zenith proximity or nearness in lands of latitudes, because the proximity to the zenith remains there many days, and the day is manifestly longer than the night. And the permanent cause, even if weak, may be more effective than the non-permanent, even if strong, like iron in a gentle fire for a while and in a strong fire for a moment. And secondly: The time of the sun's arrival at the beginning of Cancer is winter for the equator because the sun is at the utmost distance from their zenith, and summer for a locality whose latitude is forty-seven times the total declination, like the town of Saray, because it is at the utmost proximity to it, while the distance from the zenith of the two localities is equal. So the winter heat of the equator is like the summer heat of this town, even more if you consider, because before this state they have causes of heat, and the townspeople have causes of cold. And if their winter heat is like this, what do you think of their summer heat? Its answer: Preventing the similarity of the heat of the two seasons in the two localities. It would only follow if the cause of heat were confined to the nearness of the sun from the zenith, which is impossible. So it is possible that the summer heat of the supposed town becomes intense due to the increase of day length over night to nearly double, because its day length reaches about sixteen hours, and its night length about eight hours, unlike the equator, where night and day are always equal, so heat and cold are balanced. Also, what is familiar has no effect. Perhaps the people of the equator, due to their familiarity with heat, their temperaments are not affected, nor do they become heated from the heat of the sun's zenith proximity, and they consider the air cold when the zenith is distant – I mean when the sun is at the solstices – so balance remains, unlike the supposed town, where heat intensifies on their bodies and affects them due to unfamiliarity with it, and due to their transition to it from intense cold, even if gradual. And it is not hidden from the author the weakness of this answer, and likewise attributing the town's heat to terrestrial causes.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents two objections regarding the heat of equatorial regions and their refutations. The first objection claims that because the sun is always at or near the zenith at the equator, the heat there is excessive. The answer explains that the sun's passage through the zenith is very brief, while in regions at higher latitudes, the sun remains near the zenith for many days, causing greater cumulative heating. A permanent but weak cause (like a long exposure to moderate heat) can be more effective than a strong but brief one, like iron in a gentle fire for a long time versus a strong fire for an instant. The second objection compares winter at the equator (when the sun is farthest from the zenith) to summer at a latitude of 47° (like the town of Saray), arguing that the equatorial winter is as hot as that summer. The answer denies the similarity of the two seasons in the two locations. Even if the distance from the zenith is the same, the cause of heat is not limited to that distance; day length also matters. At high latitudes, days are much longer in summer (e.g., 16 hours of daylight) while nights are short, intensifying heat. At the equator, day and night are always equal, balancing heat and cold. Furthermore, habituation plays a role: equatorial people are accustomed to heat, so their bodies are less affected, whereas at higher latitudes, people experience a sudden transition from intense cold to heat, making it more impactful. The author notes that this answer is weak and attributes the heat to terrestrial causes.

Key Terms
المسامتة
السبب الدائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Constant direct sunlight always produces the most heat.
Correction: The duration of exposure is crucial; prolonged near-zenith sunlight at higher latitudes can cause more heating than brief zenith passage at the equator.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, why does the sun's zenith proximity at the equator cause less heating than at higher latitudes?
A) Because the sun is weaker at the equator.
B) Because the sun passes through the zenith quickly, while at higher latitudes it stays near the zenith for many days.
C) Because the equator has longer nights.
D) Because the air at the equator is already saturated with heat.

Key Takeaway
A permanent but weak cause can outweigh a strong but brief one, explaining why equatorial heat is not extreme despite constant zenith proximity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a brief, intense effort (like a last-minute study session) and neglected consistent, moderate work over time? Which approach did the principle in this lesson prove more effective?
?
In your pursuit of knowledge or good deeds, do you tend to seek short, dramatic moments of inspiration or steady, continuous practice? Why might the 'permanent weak cause' be superior?
?
Consider a habit you want to change. Are you waiting for a 'strong fire' moment to fix it, or can you commit to a 'gentle fire' of daily discipline? What would that look like starting today?
